==PAGE:8==
Abstract
This thesis presents the development and validation (theoretical and experimental) of
Timoshenko and Viscoelastic finite element models applied to rotor-dynamics. The
aim was to compare the contributions of shear effects, internal viscoelastic damping
and rotational bending effects to the dynamic response of flexible rotors with different
journal bearing configurations when compared to the response obtained with the
Euler-Bernoulli finite element formulation. The theoretical validations of the finite
rotor element formulations were done by reproducing the results of standard test cases
from reputable publications with the aid of a custom made MATLABTM script. The
standard test cases were taken from Nelson and McVaugh (1976) for Euler-Bernoulli
finite element, Nelson (1980) for Timoshenko finite element and Ganguly et al. (2018a)
for viscoelastic Timoshenko. Afterwards, the three models were set to reproduce the
theoretical results of a rotor-bearing system presented by Molsen and Pedersen (2014)
and adjusted to match the experimental results reported in the same publication.
Once the models were adjusted to the experimental baseline, the dynamic response
(critical speeds, unbalance response and stability map) of the rotor-bearing array
was computed for different configurations of journal bearings, selected from Someya
(1988). A comparison of the rotor dynamic response with the different models (Euler-
Bernoulli, Timoshenko and viscoelastic Timoshenko) was made. From this analysis,
it was possible to validate that the contributions of shear effects are negligible for
slender shaft configurations, since the Euler-Bernoulli dynamic response for all the
journal bearing configurations was identical to the dynamic response computed with
the Timoshenko finite element. The validation corresponding to the viscoelastic finite
element formulation was done partially, due to the limited literature available for
its implementation. In this case, the dynamic response with the viscoelastic finite
element for the different hydrodynamic bearing configurations resulted in lower values
of critical speeds when compared to the other models and an unstable unbalance
response, possibly due to overdamping.

==PAGE:10==
Preface
This thesis was prepared at the department of Mechanical Engineering of the Tech-
nical University of Denmark in fulfillment of the requirements for earning the degree
in Master of Science and Engineering within the Engineering Design and Applied
Mechanics program.
The core motivation of this project has been to develop and validate finite element
models applied to rotor-dynamics based on the Timoshenko beam theory, which ac-
counts for the shear deformation and rotational bending effects. These effects are
neglected in the traditional approach (Euler-Bernoulli), however they become very
relevant for thick shaft geometries and for operation conditions at high rotational
speed applications such as mechanical batteries (flywheel systems).
Kongens Lyngby, May 10, 2019
Salvador Gerardo Álvarez Villaseñor - s171825

==PAGE:20==
CHAPTER 1
Introduction
1.1
Background
The classic finite element formulation for rotor dynamics was originally described by
Ruhl (1970), which used this method to study the stability and unbalance response
of a turbo-rotor. In this approach, the effects of elastic bending and translational
kinetic energy were considered. This model was later expanded by Nelson and Mc-
Vaugh (1976) who considered the effects of rotatory inertia, gyroscopic moments and
axial load. These effects become very relevant at high rotational speeds or for very
thick shaft configurations. Similarly, the formulation which considered the effects of
distributed shaft eccentricity was developed by Gasch (1976).
More recently the Maxwell–Wiechert model was adapted by Golla and Hughes (1985)
to account for the effects of linear viscoelasticity within the finite element, with the
advantage, that this approach, is versatile for frequency and time domain analysis.
Ganguly et al. (2018a) introduced a viscoelastic Timoshenko shaft finite element that
incorporates the Maxwell–Wiechert model into the finite element analysis of rotors.
The resulting finite element model is suitable to compute frequency response, stability
analysis and transient response analysis of simply supported viscoelastic rotor–disc
systems. This model assumes that the viscoelastic material is incompressible and the
necessary constitutive relations are imposed in the frequency domain. The storage
modulus and loss coefficient for the viscoelastic materials are obtained experimentally,
Ganguly et al. (2018a).
1.2
Research Question
How relevant are the contributions of shear effects, internal viscoelastic damping and
rotational bending effects to the dynamic response of flexible rotors with different
journal bearing configurations when compared to the dynamic response obtained
with the traditional finite rotor element approach (Euler-Bernoulli)?
==PAGE:21==
2
1 Introduction
1.3
Scope
The scope of this project is to develop Finite Element Models applied to rotor-
dynamics. This involves a theoretical analysis of mechanical and mathematical mod-
els of shaft elements based in the Euler-Bernoulli beam theory and Timoshenko beam
theory, including evaluation of internal damping. The theoretical results would be
validated using data obtained from experimental modal analysis. Furthermore, the
project includes an evaluation of the shaft elements critical speed, stability and un-
balance response with different hydrodynamic bearings which would be added to the
mathematical models as stiffness and damping elements.
The specific objectives of this project can be summarized as follows:
1. Develop a Finite Element Model of a shaft element using the euler-Bernouli
beam theory.
2. Develop a Finite Element Model of a shaft element using the Timoshenko beam
theory.
3. Apply the Maxwell-Wiechert model introduced in Ganguly et al. (2018a) to
the Timoshenko beam elements in order to evaluate internal damping between
elastic and viscous elements.
4. Make a theoretical analysis of the Euler-Bernouli and Timoshenko based models.
5. Validate the mathematical model based in Timoshenko through data acquired
from experimental modal analysis of a shaft element.
6. Adjust the mathematical model based in Timoshenko or explain discrepancies.
7. Model different hydrodynamic bearing configurations from Someya (1988) as
stiffness and damping elements.
8. Determine the critical speeds of the shaft element for the different bearing
configurations.
9. Evaluate the bearing stability.
10. Evaluate the shaft unbalance response with the different bearing configurations.
1.4
Thesis Outline
The content of the present Thesis is divided into five chapters.
In Chapter One,
the background of the thesis is explained in general terms, the research question
presented, the scope of the thesis defined, and the present section included. In Chapter
Two, the theoretical framework of the relevant topics is presented. First, the basic
==PAGE:22==
1.4 Thesis Outline
3
finite element rotor, the Euler-Bernoulli Model, is introduced, this is mainly a review
of the material covered in the second half of the course Dynamics of Machinery
(41514) offered at DTU, which is the foundation for this work, and is documented
in Ferreira Santos. As well, the paper by Nelson and McVaugh (1976) is consulted
extensively.
Further, the theoretical background of the Timoshenko Finite Rotor
Element is developed based in Nelson (1980). This is followed by the introduction of
the Viscoelastic Timoshenko Finite Element which is based on the Maxwell-Wiechert
Model (Ganguly et al. (2018a)). Similarly, the procedure to model the rotor journal
bearings as spring and damping elements is developed based on the approach by
Someya (1988). In Chapter Three, the methodology for the theoretical validation of
the Euler-Bernoulli, Timoshenko and Viscoelastic Timoshenko Finite Rotor Element
Models is described. Similarly, a method to utilize the results reported by Molsen and
Pedersen (2014) as a baseline for model adjustment is detailed. As well, a selection
of journal bearing tables from Someya (1988) for the adjusted rotor-bearing system
Finite Element Models is defined. In Chapter Four, the results of the theoretical
validations of the Euler-Bernoulli, Timoshenko and Viscoelastic Finite Rotor Element
models are presented.
As well, the match of the experimental model adjustment
is also included.
In addition, the dynamic response of the adjusted rotor-bearing
system introduced by Molsen and Pedersen (2014) with the selected bearings is shown.
Finally, the summary, conclusions and future work recommendations regarding this
Thesis are included in Chapter Five.

==PAGE:24==
CHAPTER 2
Theory
2.1
Dynamics of Rotor-Bearing systems (Euler-Bernoulli
Finite Element)
2.1.1
Assumptions and simplifications
The typical rotor-bearing system consists of an array of these components: flexible
shaft, rigid discs, and bearings. The equations to describe the dynamics (deformation
and motion) of these components individually are developed in the following sections,
and further the equations of the assembled system are presented. These equations
are based in the assumptions and simplifications listed below:
1. The shaft is a flexible body, and as such bending is possible.
2. The shaft is a solid body with no cracks or cavities.
3. No shaft deformations occur in the longitudinal (X-axis) direction.
4. The shaft is considered to have an isotropic linear elastic material properties.
5. Bearings are assumed to be linear and massless.
6. Disc(s) mounted on the shaft are considered as a rigid body.
7. The rigid disc center line is co-linear and coincident with the flexible shaft center
line.
==PAGE:25==
6
2 Theory

represents the whirl speed and Ωrepresents the spin speed.
The coordinates of a point at any given location of the finite rotor element can be
described relative to the F reference frame by the linear displacements V (s, t) and
W(s, t) in the Y and Z directions, respectively. And by small angle rotations B(s, t)
and Γ(s, t) about the Z and Y axis, respectively. Both linear displacements and both
angle rotations may be grouped into a displacement vector {q}.

2.1.6

2.2 Dynamics of Rotor-Bearing systems (Timoshenko Finite Rotor Element)

2.2
Dynamics of Rotor-Bearing systems (Timoshenko
Finite Rotor Element)
2.2.1
Mechanical model, assumptions and simplifications
Figure 2.5: Total deformation in the XY plane: shear+bending deformations
In a Timoshenko finite rotor element the total deformation is the sum of two parts:
bending and shear energy (see Figure 2.5). This approach is very relevant when the
transverse dimensions of the beam are not negligible compared to its axial length (i.e.
thick shafts). This proportion is defined by the slenderness ratio R = r/2L, which is
a dimensionless parameter.
In the section below, the development of the equations of motion for a Timoshenko
finite rotor element is presented based in Nelson (1980).
2.2.2
Timoshenko Finite Rotor Element Equation of Motion
Similarly to the Euler-Bernoulli Finite Rotor Element, the translation of the Tim-
oshenko Finite Rotor Element cross-section center line is given by the two linear
displacements (V, W), however in this case, (V, W) have two components: a contribu-
tion due to bending (Vβ,Wβ) and a contribution due to shear deformation (Vσ,Wσ).
As in section 2.1.2, the rotations of the cross-section are described by the rotation
angles (B = −∂Wβ/∂s,Γ = ∂Vβ/∂s) which are associated with the bending deforma-
tion of the element.
The linear displacements of a typical point internal to the element are approximated
by equation 2.9. However, the individual shape functions are of the form:
ψr(s) =
1
1 + Φ[αr(s) + Φβr(s)];
r = 1, 2, 3, 4
(2.40)
==PAGE:37==
18
2 Theory
the αi(s) functions are associated with the bending deformation of a Timoshenko
beam and the βi(s) functions are associated with the shear deformation.
These
functions are given by:
ν = s

2.3
Dynamics of Rotor-Bearing systems (Viscoelastic
Timoshenko Finite Element)
2.3.1
Mechanical model, assumptions and simplifications
The Maxwell–Wiechert model is used to model linear viscoelasticity. It is suitable
for frequency domain and time domain formulations. The Golla and Hughes (1985)
method uses a modified Maxwell–Wiechert model which is especially suitable for using
with finite element analysis. Physically, this method associates small masses with the
==PAGE:39==
20
2 Theory
additional over damped oscillators constituting the parallel Maxwell branches. The
inertia, stiffness, and damping of the oscillators is determined from experimental data.
In the Viscoelastic Timoshenko Finite Element model the material is assumed to
be incompressible and necessary constitutive relations are imposed in the frequency
domain.
2.3.2
Stress-strain relations for viscoelastic Timoshenko beam
Figure 2.6: Maxwell-Wiechert model with 3 parallel branches considering deformation
in 2 planes (XY and XZ)
Figure 2.6 shows a Maxwell–Wiechert model with 3 parallel branches. Ganguly et al.
(2018a) reported that the Maxwell–Wiechert model with three parallel branches had
an accurate match with the experimental data. However, for simplicity a Maxwell–
Wiechert model with a single Maxwell branch is considered first and the principle
for multiple branches is extended afterwards. A Maxwell branch here represents the
relation between normal stress and strain, and the relation between two shear stresses
and corresponding shear strains. The stress–strain relations are:
σ = Eεc + ηε1( ˙εc −˙εb1)
τxy = Gγxyc + ηγxy1(˙γxyc −˙γxyb1)
τxz = Gγxzc + ηγxz1(˙γxzc −˙γxzb1)
(2.49)
with the constraint relations,
ηε1( ˙εc −˙εb1) = E1εb1
ηγxy1(˙γxyc −˙γxyb1) = G1γxyb1
ηγxz1(˙γxzc −˙γxzb1) = G1γxzb1
(2.50)
The subscripts notation is: ”c” and ”b” are used for actual strains and additional
strains corresponding to internal damping variables, respectively.

2.3.4
Spring stiffness and damping coefficients Extraction for the
parallel Maxwell elements
For viscoelastic materials, frequency sweep tests are done in a dynamic mechanical
spectroscope to obtain the values of storage modulus (Es) and loss modulus (EI) at
different frequencies. Then the spring stiffness and damping coefficients of the parallel
branches of the Maxwell–Wiechert model are calculated by curve fitting, using a
nonlinear least squares technique (see Recktenwald (2000)).
2.3.5
Frequency Response of Viscoelastic Timoshenko Rotor
There are two methods for a frequency domain analysis: (1) conventional and (2)
state space analysis:
• Conventional Analysis:
For this approach the following finite element equations without any damping
DoFs are given
==PAGE:45==
26
2 Theory
[M]{¨q} + [G]{ ˙q} + E[ ¨K]{q} + [K1]{q} = {F}
(2.68)
Assuming harmonic excitation in the form {F} = {F0}ejωt, the displacement
and complex material modulus are written as follows:
{q} = {q0}ejωt
(2.69)
E = Es(ω)(1 + jη(ω))
(2.70)
where
E = ES(ω) + jEI(ω)
where
EI(ω) = Es(ω)η(ω)
(2.71)
The material modulus is expressed as a frequency-dependent complex number in
terms of storage modulus and loss coefficient. As can be noticed, this method
requires the knowledge of the corresponding viscoelastic material parameters
curve fit function, or the value of the experimental data points (from section
2.3.4). The displacement {q0} is a complex number and can be determined from
the following equation
{q0} = {Es(ω)(1 + jη(ω))[ ¯K] −ω2[M] + jω[G]}−1{F0}
(2.72)
• State Space Analysis: This method utilizes the finite element equations (i.e.
equations 2.66 and 2.67) proposed in the previous sections. Each of these equa-
tions can be assembled and written as follows
[A]{ ˙Z} + [B]{Z} = {R}
(2.73)
Considering {R} = {R0}eiωt and {Z} = {Z0}eiωt, one obtains:
{Z0} = (jω[A] + [B])−1{R0}
(2.74)
The matrices [A] and [B] are not frequency (ω) dependent.
==PAGE:46==
2.4 Modeling of Hydrodynamic Bearings
27
2.4
Modeling of Hydrodynamic Bearings
In section 2.1.4 the bearing equation of motion was defined and it was established
that in the field of rotor dynamics bearings are modelled as stiffness and damping
elements. This is the case of journal or hydrodynamic bearings. However, the values
of the stiffness and damping are not constant, instead these depend on the geometry
of the selected bearing, shaft, and fluid film properties. In the following sections the
assumptions made to model the bearing oil film coefficients, the main dimensions of
journal bearings, and the definition of bearing coefficients as introduced by Someya
(1988) are presented.
2.4.1
Assumptions for Modelling of Journal Bearings
In this section, the following assumptions are made:
1. The bearing operates under fluid film lubrication.
2. The lubricant is incompressible and Newtonian.
3. No deformation of the bearing or the journal takes place. Their axes remain
parallel.
4. The flow of lubricant is isothermal, isoviscous, laminar and free of inertia, unless
it is defined otherwise.
5. Vibration of the journal occurs in the vicinity of its static equilibrium, and its
amplitude is very small compared with the bearing clearance for the oil film
force to be linear.
==PAGE:47==
28
2 Theory
2.4.2
Journal Bearings Dimensions and Governing Parameters
Figure 2.7: hydrodynamic effect of journal bearing
The sliding surface of a journal bearing usually consists of a cylinder (see Figure 2.7),
the following main dimensions are generally used for its description:
• D = 2R: Inner bearing diameter [m]
• R: Inner bearing radius or radius of inner cylindrical surface [m]
• r: Journal radius [m]
• Cp = R −r: Machined clearance, usually taken as the representative bearing
clearance [m]
• Cb: assembled clearance. For 360◦cylindrical bearing C = Cp = Cb = R −r
• L: bearing length [m]
From these dimensions the following dimensionless geometrical parameters, which
govern the characteristics of the bearing, are defined:
==PAGE:48==
2.4 Modeling of Hydrodynamic Bearings
29
• L : D: length-to-diameter ratio [-]
• ψ: Clearance ratio [-]
ψ = Cp
R
(2.75)
• mp: preload factor [-]
mp = 1 −Cb
Cp
(2.76)
• e: eccentricity [m]
• ε: eccentricity ratio [-]
ε = e/Cb
(2.77)
• ϕ: attitude angle [rad] - Angle between vertical and radial line from bearing
center to journal center.
• ω: velocity of journal center/whirl speed [rad/s]
• Ω: Spin speed [rad/s]
• N: Spin speed (Ω= 2πN) [r.p.m.]
• S: Sommerfeld number [-]
S = µoilNLD
W
( R
Cp
)
(2.78)
where µoil is the oil film viscosity [Pa ∗s] and W is the bearing load [N].
2.4.3
Dynamic Characteristics of Journal Bearings
When the journal vibrates, squeeze film pressure and wedge film pressure are gener-
ated. These pressures give rise to spring and damping forces of oil film and therefore
influence the onset speed of the whirling, the critical speeds and amplitude of the ro-
tor system. Additionally, when the centre of a journal supported by a journal bearing
moves within the clearance circle, the resultant force of oil film pressure acting on the
journal varies. The definition and relationship between oil film forces and damping
and stiffness effects is presented below.
The components Fx and Fy of oil film force are expressed as functions of coordinates
x and y of the journal centre and its velocity components ¨x and ¨y:
==PAGE:49==
30
2 Theory
Fx = Fx(x, y, ¨x, ¨y),
Fy = Fy(x, y, ¨x, ¨y)
(N)
(2.79)
The functions of eq. 2.79 can be linearized by the Taylor series of the first order when
the assumptions of vibration of the journal occurring in the vicinity of the static
equilibrium position and of very small amplitude and velocity hold. Thus, oil film
force can be rewritten as:
F =
[Fx
Fy
]
(2.80)
we get the following equations:

oil film stiffness and damping matrices (size [2x2]), respectively. These are defined as
follows:

3.1
Theoretical Validation
3.1.1
Validation of Euler-Bernoulli Finite Element Model
Figure 3.1: Rotor-bearing system configuration, Nelson and McVaugh (1976)
The approach of this thesis for the theoretical validation of the Euler-Bernoulli Fi-
nite Element model was to implement the equations and matrices presented in sec-
tion 2.1.2 in a MATLAB script to replicate the results published by Nelson and
McVaugh (1976).In the latter publication, the finite element method was used to
compute the whirl speeds and unbalance response of a rotor bearing system (see Fig-
ure 3.1), with two different bearing configurations. The material used for the rotor
was steel, (ρsteel = 7800kg/m3 and E = 2078E11N/m2). The rotor geometry had
a variable cross section, and with the aim to simplify the computation effort, the
coordinate reduction method introduced in section 2.1.6 was implemented. Firstly,
the rotor was discretized into 17 subelements (18 nodes) and as a result of the coordi-
nate reduction the simplified rotor could be represented by only 6 elements (7 nodes).
Nelson and McVaugh (1976) presented the geometry of the rotor in tabular form and
is reproduced in this thesis in Table 3.1.

3.1.2
Validation of Timoshenko Finite Element Model
Figure 3.3: Sketch of Non-rotating Timo-
shenko shaft
Figure 3.4:
Sketch of Rotating Timo-
shenko shaft
The theoretical validation of the Timoshenko Finite Element model was done by
modelling a rotor-bearing system of length 0.5 m with the configuration and scenar-
ios presented by Nelson (1980) with the aid of a custom made MATLAB script. And
by comparing the obtained results with the reported results. In their study, Nelson
==PAGE:54==
3.1 Theoretical Validation
35
(1980) analyzed the accuracy of the Timoshenko beam finite rotating shaft element
for two different scenarios and compared their results to those from other reputable
sources. In the first scenario the first and second natural frequencies of a uniform
non-rotating simply supported beam, λ = 0, with varying slenderness ratio were com-
puted and compared to those of the Timoshenko beam theory published by Dym
and Shames (1973). The results of the comparison were presented in tabular and
graphical form. The results are reproduced in this thesis and shown in Table 3.4 and
plotted in Figure 3.5 for the first natural frequency, and in Figure 3.6 for the second
natural frequency, respectively. In the second scenario, Nelson (1980) computed the
primary forward and backward critical speeds, λ = ±1, for the same beam, with vary-
ing slenderness ratio, and compared the results to those published by Eshleman and
Eubanks (1969). Similarly, the results were presented in tabular and graphical form
and reproduced in this thesis in Table 3.5 and, as in the previous scenario, presented
graphically in Figure 3.7 for the first critical speed and in Figure 3.8 for the second
critical speed, respectively.
In their study Nelson (1980) conducted the analysis with different discretizations (in-
cluded in Tables 3.4 and 3.5). However, only the comparison with 5 finite element
discretization was plotted. In this Thesis the same approach was kept. In their arti-
cle, Nelson (1980) utilized the following notation for their tables, which was kept in
this thesis for clarity and consistency purposes: p1,2,3 represented the values of p from
equations 7.70, 7.71,7.75, respectively from Dym and Shames (1973), p4,5 represented
the values of p from closed form solutions, computed through equations 41 and 42
from Eshleman and Eubanks (1969) and p(n) represented the value of p using n finite
elements.
The non-dimensional natural frequency (p) can be computed with the following ex-
pression:
p4 = AρΩ2L4
EI
(3.1)
where,
• A - shaft cross section area [m2]
• ρ - shaft material density [kg/m3]
• Ω- shaft spin speed [rad/s]
• L - shaft length [m]
• E - shaft material modulus of elasticity [Pa]
• I - shaft cross section moment of inertia [m4]

3.1.3
Validation of the Viscoelastic Timoshenko Finite Element
Model
Figure 3.9:
Rotor-disc model I (not to
scale), Ganguly et al. (2018a)
Figure 3.10: Rotor-disc model II (not to
scale), Ganguly et al. (2018a)
As it was done in both of the previous sections, the theoretical validation of the
==PAGE:58==
3.1 Theoretical Validation
39
viscoleastic Timoshenko finite Element model was done by reproducing the results
presented by Ganguly et al. (2018a). In their study, three aspects of two different
rotor–disk models were analyzed. These were: a) frequency response, b) stability
analysis and c) Time response analysis. In the present thesis, however, it was only
possible to approximate the results related to the frequency response for both rotor-
bearing systems, since the boundary conditions necessary to compute the particular
solution of the system ordinary differential equations were not specified in the study,
and further documentation was not found.
The first rotor-bearing system (Model I) consisted of a uniform simply supported
rotor with a single rigid disc mounted, while Model II had two rigid discs mounted.
Similarly, the study was done considering two different materials: Acrylonitrile Bu-
tadiene Styrene (ABS) and Polypropylene carbonate (PPC).
The spin speed was kept at zero. A vertical harmonic force of amplitude 1 N and
varying frequency was applied at the location of the single disc in the first model
and the left disc in the second model. A Maxwell-Wiechert model with tree Maxwell
branches was used.
The dimensions of the rotor were 600mm length and 60mm diameter, see Figure 3.9.
For Model I, the disc had a mass of 1 kg and 100 mm radius of gyration for polar
moment of inertia. The rotor shaft was simply supported with the disc located at a
distance of 400 mm from the left end support (not centered).
Model II had the same rotor dimensions as Model I and was fitted with two identical
discs, see Figure 3.10. Both discs had a mass of 1 kg and 100 mm radius of gyration
corresponding to polar moment of inertia. The rotor shaft was simply supported with
the discs placed at an even distance between themselves and from the supports of 200
mm.
The frequency response function of both systems was obtained using equation 2.74
and compared with the plots presented by Ganguly et al. (2018a), see Figures 3.11
and 3.12. In contrast to the reference study, in this thesis it was not possible to
obtain the conventional response from equation 2.72 since the experimental data
points corresponding to the materials storage and loss modulus were not provided by
the authors. The frequency response for both rotor models were plotted by Ganguly
et al. (2018a), Figures 3.11 and 3.12. However data in tabular form was not available,
therefore the validation process was limited to analyze the position of the resonance
peaks for the computed Finite Element.
The parameters necessary to characterize the material viscoelastic behavior are pre-
sented in table 3.6 for ABS and PPC.

3.2 Finite Element Model Adjustment to Experimental Data
41
3.2
Finite Element Model Adjustment to Experimental
Data
Figure 3.13: Rotor setup used by Molsen
and Pedersen (2014) for theoretical and ex-
perimental validation
Figure 3.14: Sketch of rotor setup used by
Molsen and Pedersen (2014) for theoretical
and experimental validation (not to scale)
The final step in the validation process of the finite element model developed in this
thesis consisted in reproducing the theoretical and experimental results reported in
the work by Molsen and Pedersen (2014). In their study, the authors made a theo-
retical and experimental analysis of the stability of a a rotor-bearing system, which
consisted of a rotor of length 0.3 m mounted on a couple of hydrodynamic bearings
(two-axial-groove, L/D=1.0).
The study was divided in four parts: In the first part, the authors computed the the-
oretical response of a rotor-bearing system (Euler-Bernoulli Finite Element Model)
under four different scenarios. In each of these scenarios the authors changed pa-
rameters of the rotor-bearing system such as shaft diameter, bearing lubricant fluid,
number and size of discs, bearing diameter and spacing between bearings, with the
aim of finding an optimal configuration in terms of stability. From this stage, the
fourth setup was selected as the most suitable. This setup, consisted of a steel rotor
of length 0.3 m, 15 mm uniform diameter, two centered and equally spaced hydrody-
namical bearings, and two discs - one mounted at the center of the rotor with 180 mm
diameter and 12 mm thickness. The second disc consisted of an aluminum measuring
disc mounted in the left end of the rotor. The dimensions of the disc were 100 mm
diameter and 8 mm thickness. The response of this setup was predicted considering
cutting/cooling fluid.
The second part consists of the experimental setup to experimentally determine the
viscosity of an emulsion made of water an 5% of cutting fluid (Blasocut BC 25-MD).
This emulsion was used during the experimental part of the study, since the cutting
fluid properties prevented any corrosion against stainless steel and brass, which were
the materials from which the rotor and bearings were built. The viscosity of the water
and cutting fluid emulsion was found to be 1.140966e−03±2.161157e−10
m∗s/m2.
==PAGE:61==
42
3 Methodology
The third part of the study, consisted of a finite element analysis using PTC Creo
to analyze the deformations of the bearing housing with two different materials (i.e.
Acrylic glass and aluminum). Molsen and Pedersen (2014) concluded that aluminum
bearing housings were better suited for the experimental setup since the bearing hous-
ings made of this material have less deformations when compared to those made from
acrylic glass.
For the fourth part of the study, the authors computed the theoretical response of
the selected rotor-bearing setup, considering the bearing lubricant fluid viscosity com-
puted in part two, and using aluminum bearing housings. At the same time, they
made a transient analysis to determine the rotor-bearing system response experimen-
tally. Finally, the authors tried to match the theoretical and experimental results
by adjusting the bearing clearance within the components (i.e. rotor and bearings)
specifications. However they found that the match between the results provided by
the MATLAB model and those obtained experimentally was only achievable when
considering a bearing clearance of 70
µm, which was not in the specification. It is
expected in the present thesis to match the results from the study by Molsen and Ped-
ersen (2014) with the aid of a MATLAB script for the Euler-Bernoulli, Timoshenko
and Viscoelastic Timoshenko rotors. The results reported by Molsen and Pedersen
(2014) are summarized in Table 3.7:
Table 3.7: Summary of results from Molsen and Pedersen (2014)

3.3 Modelling of Rotor Response with Different Bearing Configurations

3.3
Modelling of Rotor Response with Different Bearing

After the MATLAB script models for the Euler-Bernoulli, Timoshenko and Viscoelas-
tic Timoshenko finite element rotors were adjusted to match the experimental results
reported by Molsen and Pedersen (2014), the same rotor-bearing configuration was
used to model the system response with different hydrodynamic bearing configura-
tions. The aim was to evaluate the contributions of shear deformation and internal
viscoelastic damping when compared to the response of an Euler-Bernoulli rotor.
As in the previous section, the evaluation of the system response involved the deter-
mination of the rotor first and second Critical speeds through the use of Campbell’s
diagram. The computation of the system unbalance response, and the analysis of the
system stability map. In addition, the mode shapes for the first and second critical
speeds as that of the stability threshold were provided.
The bearing tables were selected from Someya (1988). To keep consistency with the
previous steps of the validation, only bearing tables with a bearing diameter to width
ratio of L/D = 1.0 were chosen. These were:
1. CALCULATION 02: Two-axial-groove bearing, L/D = 1.0
2. CALCULATION 04: Four-axial-groove bearing, L/D = 1.0
3. CALCULATION 05: Cylindrical with pressure dam, L/D = 1.0
4. CALCULATION 08: Partial arc 120°, L/D = 1.0
5. CALCULATION 09: Partial arc 150°, L/D = 1.0
6. CALCULATION 13: Two-lobe bearing, L/D = 1.0, mp = 1/2
7. CALCULATION 14: Two-lobe bearing, L/D = 1.0, mp = 2/3
8. CALCULATION 15: Two-lobe bearing, L/D = 1.0, mp = 3/4
9. CALCULATION 28: Four-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 0, LBP
10. CALCULATION 29: Four-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 1/2, LBP
11. CALCULATION 30: Four-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 2/3, LBP
12. CALCULATION 31: Four-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 3/4, LBP
13. CALCULATION 37: Four-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 1/2, LOP
14. CALCULATION 38: Four-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 2/3, LOP
==PAGE:63==
44
3 Methodology
15. CALCULATION 39: Four-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 3/4, LOP
16. CALCULATION 44: Five-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 0, LBP
17. CALCULATION 45: Five-Pad tilting pad, L/D = 1.0, mp = 0.5, LBP

4 Results and Discussion
The results computed with the aid of the MATLAB script had a good agreement to
those reported by Nelson and McVaugh (1976). The results corresponding to the first
bearing configuration (symmetrical bearings) were presented graphically (blue solid
line) in Figure 4.1. To facilitate the comparison, the results from Nelson and Mc-
Vaugh (1976) were plotted as well (black dashed line). It was observed that the finite
element model computations for the first whirl speed were below to those reported by
Nelson and McVaugh (1976). The second and third whirl speed curves showed good
agreement with the results from cited publication.
The computation results corresponding to the second bearing configuration are pre-
sented in Figure 4.2.
The finite element model shows a good match for the first
backward and forward whirl speed. For the second whirl speed, the final element com-
putations were above the reported results, however the corresponding curves showed
the same signature. For the third whirl speed, the finite element computations were
also above the values reported by Nelson and McVaugh (1976). However the trajecto-
ries of the curves had an adequate agreement with those reported. The discrepancies
between the finite element models results may be explained by the differences in
computation capacity available at the time of the study development.

In Figure 4.3 the values of the first natural frequency for a uniform simply supported
Timoshenko beam of varying slenderness ratio were presented. The results from the
present thesis were represented with blue markers in the plot, while the results re-
ported by Nelson (1980) were presented in black. It was observed that the values
for the pure Euler-Bernoulli and Rayleigh beam computed with the MATLAB based
script had a good agreement with the reference values. For the case of a beam with
shear effects and for the full Timoshenko beam, the computed values were slightly
below the reference values. This deviation became more apparent with an increasing
slenderness ratio.
==PAGE:67==
48
4 Results and Discussion
Similarly, in Figure 4.6 the values of the second natural frequency for a uniform
simply supported Timoshenko beam of varying slenderness ratio were presented. As
in the previous Figure, it was observed that the computed values for the pure Euler-
Bernoulli and Rayleigh beam had a good agreement with the reference values. For the
case of the beam with shear effects and for the full Timoshenko model, the computed
values were slightly below the reference values, with the deviation increasing with the
slenderness ratio. It was of particular interest to note that the computed values for a
beam with shear effects had a perfect match with the corresponding values from the
closed form solution.

In Figure 4.5 the values of the first critical speeds for a uniform simply supported
Timoshenko rotor of varying slenderness ratios were presented. It was observed that
the computed values for the forward critical speeds had an adequate agreement with
the reference values, with a slight deviation, which became more apparent with an
increasing slenderness ratio. The values for the first backward critical speed had a
very good agreement with the reported values. Both, the computed and reference
values for the first backward critical speed where bigger when compared to the values
of the closed form solutions.
Similarly, in Figure 4.6 the values of the second critical speed for a uniform simply
==PAGE:68==
4.1 Theoretical Validation
49
supported Timoshenko rotor for various slenderness ratios were presented. It was
observed that the computed values for the backward second critical had an exact
match with the reference values, while the values corresponding to the forward sec-
ond critical speed had a good match to the reference values at low slenderness ratios
and deviated from the reference values at high slenderness ratios. The finite element
reference values for the forward speed had a good match with those from the closed
form solution.
4.1.3
Validation of the Viscoelastic Timoshenko Finite Element
Model
The state space frequency response for the rotor-bearing systems introduced in section
3.1.3 were presented in Figure 4.8 for Model I, and in Figure 4.10 for Model II,
respectively. The plots presented by Ganguly et al. (2018a) were placed to the left
to facilitate the comparison of results. For Model I (Figure 4.8 vs Figure 4.7), the
resonance frequency for ABS rotor material (top plot) was found at 364.42 rad/s,
which was within the range of 0-500 rad/s of resonance (1) shown in Figure 4.7. The
anti resonance peak was found at 1294.34 rad/s, which was within the range of 1000-
1500 rad/s of anti resonance (2) from the reference plot. For the PPC rotor material
(bottom plot) the resonance frequency was found at 326.73 rad/s, which was within
the range of 0-500 rad/s of resonance (1) shown in Figure 4.7. The anti resonance peak
was found at 1181.24 rad/s, which was within the range of 1000-1500 rad/s of anti
resonance (2) from the reference plot, and a second resonance was located at 1445.13
rad/s which was also close to 1500 rad/s as in the second resonance peak (3) of the
reference plot. For both materials, the signature of the computed frequency response
plotted had a similar signature to the reference frequency response. However, the
amplitude of the computed frequency response is higher compared to the amplitude
of the reference frequency response. As well, the curves had a different behaviour at
low forcing frequency values (red arrow).

For Model II (Figure 4.10 vs Figure 4.9), the resonance frequency for ABS rotor
material (top plot) was found at 301.59 rad/s, which was within the range of 0-500
rad/s of resonance (1) shown in Figure 4.7. The anti resonance peak was found at
841.95 rad/s, which was within the range of 500-1000 rad/s of anti resonance (2) of
the reference plot. A second resonance peak was located at 1156.11 rad/s, within the
range of 1000-1500 rad/s of the resonance peak (3) shown in the reference plot. For

rad/s, which was within the range of 0-500 rad/s of resonance (1) shown in Figure 4.7.

was located at 1080.71 rad/s which was within the range of 1000-1500 rad/s as in the
second resonance peak (3) of the reference plot. For both materials, the signature
of the computed frequency response plotted had a similar signature to the reference
frequency response. However, the amplitude of the computed frequency response is
higher compared to the amplitude of the reference frequency response. As well, the
curves had a different behaviour at low forcing frequency values (red arrow).

Figure 4.11: Dynamic rotor response with Two-axial-groove, L/D = 1.0, bearing
configuration, C = 95µm
By setting the model to the theoretical parameters (Table 3.7) reported by Molsen
and Pedersen (2014), it was possible to obtain the rotor dynamic response as shown
in Figure 4.11. The following observations were made:
• There was no difference between results from Euler-Bernoulli model when com-
pared to results from Timoshenko model. This was compliant with the results
from section 4.1.2.
• Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko: The forward and backward first critical speeds
matched perfectly with the theoretical results reported. The computed first
backward critical speed had a value of 2448 r.p.m., while the reported value
was 2410. Thus, a deviation of 1.58%. Similarly, the computed first forward
critical speed had a value of 3094 r.p.m., while the reported value was 3036
r.p.m. Thus, a deviation of 1.91%.
• Viscoelastic Timoshenko: The forward and backward first critical speeds showed
an acceptable match to the theoretical results reported, both were below the
values reported by Molsen and Pedersen (2014). The computed first backward
==PAGE:72==
4.2 Finite Element Model Adjustment to Experimental Data
53
critical speed had a value of 2248 r.p.m., while the reported value was 2410
r.p.m., a deviation of -6.72%.
Similarly, the computed first forward critical
speed had a value of 2917 r.p.m., a deviation of 3.92%.
• From the unbalance response it was observed a local peak at the primary back-
ward critical speed, and the biggest amplitude located at the primary forward
critical speed. After this point, a divergence between the unbalance response
of the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko models with respect to the unbalance
response of the Timoshenko Viscoelastic model was observed.
• It was observed that all three models became unstable at 3469 r.p.m., a devia-
tion of 4.57% from the theoretical value.
After matching the theoretical results from Molsen and Pedersen (2014), the same
procedure was followed to match the experimental results reported, the tolerance
value for the bearings was set to 70µm. However, the match was not adequate. In
order to achieve a more suitable match, the density of disc 2 was set to 2700 kg/m3
(Aluminum). While there was an improvement in the computed dynamic response, it
was still not suitable, therefore several computations with smaller bearing tolerance
values were made. An adequate match was found at C = 55µm. The system response
for this bearing clearance value was shown in Figure 4.12.

The following observations were made from the previous Figure:
==PAGE:73==
54
4 Results and Discussion
• There was no difference between results from Euler-Bernoulli model when com-
pared to results from Timoshenko model.
• Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko: The backward first critical speed (3585 r.p.m.)
had an adequate match to the experimental results (3500 r.p.m.) reported, a
deviation of 2.43%. While, the forward first critical speed had a value of 4054
r.p.m., while the reported value was 4300 r.p.m., a deviation of -5.72%.
• Viscoelastic Timoshenko: The forward and backward first critical speeds showed
a poor match to the experimental results reported, both were below the values
reported by Molsen and Pedersen (2014). The first backward critical speed had
a value of 3231 r.p.m., a deviation of -7.68%. Similarly, the first forward critical
speed had a value of 3783 r.p.m., a deviation of -12.02%.
• For the unbalance response it was observed a single peak at the primary forward
critical speed value for all models. At higher values of Ωthe amplitude increased,
this clearly showed instability in the system, particularly in the case of the
viscoelastic model.
• It was observed that the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko models became unsta-
ble at 5346 r.p.m., which matched exactly with the experimental value reported.
The viscoelastic model became unstable at 4828 r.p.m., with a deviation of
-9.67% from the experimental value.
Considering the previous observations, the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko models
had an adequate adjustment to reproduce the experimental results by Molsen and
Pedersen (2014), while the viscoelastic model match was rather poor. This rotor-
bearing system configuration was kept to model the dynamic system response of the
journal bearings previously listed in section 3.3.

Figure 4.13: Dynamic rotor response with Four-axial-groove, L/D = 1.0, bearing

The dynamic response for a rotor-bearing system with Four-axial-groove, L/D = 1.0,
bearing configuration is shown in Figure 4.13. The following observations were made
from the plots shown in the figure:
• Campbell’s Diagram: The critical speed values for the Euler-Bernoulli and
Timoshenko finite element model were 3344 r.p.m (backward) and 3679 r.p.m
(forward), the range in between speeds was 335 r.p.m. In the case of the vis-
coelastic finite element model those values were 3033 r.p.m. (backward) and
3467 r.p.m. (forward), the range was 434 r.pm. The critical speed values for the
viscoelastic model were smaller compared to those of the Euler-Bernoulli and
Timoshenko finite element models. The values were -5.76% and -9.30% smaller,
for the forward and backward critical speeds, respectively.
• Unbalance Response: The unbalance response curves for the three models
follow the same signature, which consists of a main peak at the value of the
primary forward critical speed. Beyond that point the amplitude of the curves
==PAGE:75==
56
4 Results and Discussion
decreases, however at higher values of Ωthe amplitude starts to increase, pos-
sibly due to instability. The increase of amplitude at higher values of Ωwas
more pronounced for the viscoelastic finite element model.
• Stability Map: The instability speeds for all three finite element models were
very similar: 3929 r.p.m. for the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko finite element
models, and 3695 r.p.m.
for the viscoelastic finite element model.
Thus a
deviation of 5.95%.

Figure 4.14: Dynamic rotor response with Cylindrical with pressure dam, L/D = 1.0,
bearing configuration, C = 55µm
The dynamic response for a rotor-bearing system with Cylindrical with pressure dam
bearing configuration is shown in Figure 4.14. The following observations were made
from the plots shown in the figure:
• Campbell’s Diagram: The critical speed values for the Euler-Bernoulli and
Timoshenko finite element model were 3754 r.p.m (backward) and 4039 r.p.m
(forward), the range in between speeds was of 285 r.p.m. In the case of the
viscoelastic finite element model those values were 3349 r.p.m. (backward) and
3754 r.p.m. (forward), the range was 405 r.pm. The critical speed values for the
viscoelastic model were smaller compared to those of the Euler-Bernoulli and
Timoshenko finite element models. The values were -7.06% and -10.79% smaller,
for the forward and backward critical speeds, respectively. It is important to
==PAGE:76==
4.3 Modelling of Rotor Response with Different Bearing Configurations
57
note, that the values of the backward critical speed for the Euler-Bernoulli
model were identical to the value of the forward critical speed for the viscoelastic
model.
• Unbalance Response: The unbalance response curves for the three models
followed the same signature, which consisted of a main peak at the value of the
primary forward critical speed. Beyond that point the amplitude of the curves
decreased, however at higher values of Ωthe amplitude started to increase,
possibly due to instability. The increase of amplitude at higher values of Ωwas
more pronounced for the viscoelastic finite element model.
• Stability Map: The instability speeds for all three finite element models were
very close: 5439 r.p.m. for the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko finite element
models, and 5291 r.p.m.
for the viscoelastic finite element model.
Thus a
deviation of 2.72%. In fact, the curves overlap in the stable region, and once
the instability threshold was crossed the curve of the viscoelastic model diverged,
rising more steeply.

In

Figure 4.17: Dynamic rotor response with Two-lobe bearing, L/D = 1.0, mp = 1/2,
bearing configuration, C = 55µm
The dynamic response for a rotor-bearing system with Two-lobe, mp = 1/2, bearing
configuration is shown in Figure 4.17. The following observations were made from
the plots shown in the figure:
• Campbell’s Diagram: The critical speed values for the Euler-Bernoulli and
Timoshenko finite element model were 3237 r.p.m (backward) and 3644 r.p.m
(forward), respectively.
The range in between speeds was of 407 r.p.m.
In
the case of the viscoelastic finite element model those values were 2898 r.p.m.
(backward) and 3389 r.p.m. (forward), the range was 491 r.pm. The critical
speed values for the viscoelastic model were smaller compared to those of the
Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko finite element models. The values were -6.99%
and -10.47% smaller, for the forward and backward critical speeds, respectively.
• Unbalance Response: The unbalance response curves for the Euler-Bernoulli
and Timoshenko models consisted of a main peak at the value of the primary
forward critical speed. Beyond the primary forward critical speed value the
amplitude of the curves decreased, and then increased again at higher values of
Ω. For the viscoelastic model the peak was less pronounced and beyond the pri-
mary forward critical speed value the amplitude of the curves decreased slightly,
and then increased again at higher values of Ω, possibly due to instability. The
increase of amplitude at higher values of Ωwas more steep for the viscoelastic
finite element model.
==PAGE:80==

• Stability Map: The instability speeds for the three finite element models
were very similar: 5472 r.p.m. for the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko finite
element models, and 4698 r.p.m. for the viscoelastic finite element model. Thus
a deviation of -14.14%. The curves overlap for most part of the stable region,
and at approximately a y-axis value of -50 rad/s the curve of the viscoelastic
model started to diverge, rising more steeply beyond that point.

Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko finite element models. The values were -7.51%

==PAGE:81==

primary forward critical speed. Then value of the amplitude also increased at

followed a very similar signature. However the values for instability speed were
slightly divergent: 7595 r.p.m. for the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko finite

region, and at an approximate y-axis value of -180 rad/s the curves had an

finite element model the instability speed is found at 1697 r.pm.

In

(backward) and 885 r.p.m. (forward), the range was 154 r.pm. The critical
speed values for the viscoelastic model were smaller compared to those of the
Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko finite element models. The values were -19.40%
and -17.40% smaller, for the forward and backward critical speeds, respectively.
• Unbalance Response: The unbalance response curve for the Euler-Bernoulli
and Timoshenko finite element models had a double peak, the fist one (smaller)
located at approximately 1200 r.p.m., the second one (bigger) at a value of Ω
close to 4000 r.p.m. The amplitude of the unbalance response curve for the
viscoelastic model increased as the value of Ωincreased.
• Stability Map: The instability curves for the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko
finite element model did not cross the instability threshold. For the viscoelastic
finite element model the instability speed is found at 3412 r.pm.

==PAGE:97==
78
==PAGE:98==
CHAPTER 5
Conclusions,
Recommendations &
Future Work
5.1
Conclusions
In chapter two of this thesis, mechanical and mathematical models for the Euler-
Bernoulli Finite Rotor Element Model were introduced. Next, the Timoshenko Finite
Rotor Element mechanical and mathematical models were also presented to account
for the effects of shear energy in the rotor deformation. Similarly, the equations for
a Timoshenko Finite Element Rotor with internal viscoelastic effects were presented.
As well, the procedure to model hydrodynamic bearings as stiffness and damping
coefficients which depend on the Sommerfeld number was defined.
The following
general conclusions regarding these models and the rotor dynamic response were
made:
• It was possible to develop and validate a MATLAB script of an Euler-Bernoulli
Finite Element Rotor by computing the whirl speeds, within a spin speed range
of 0-120,000 r.p.m., for a typical rotor of variable cross-section and two different
bearing configurations. These results were compared to those reported by Nel-
son and McVaugh (1976), and it was found that for the case of isotropic bearings,
the results had a very tight match. While in the case of orthotropic bearing
configuration, the computed whirl speeds had some deviation with respect to
the reported values, but remained within an acceptable range.
• The MATLAB script was modified to account for shear effect contributions
(Timoshenko Finite Element), and was validated by reproducing the results of
the standard test case from Nelson (1980). The computed non-dimensional nat-
ural frequency values corresponding to the first part of the standard test case
(simply supported non-rotating Timoshenko beam), matched the reported re-
sults with great accuracy. The values computed for the second non-dimensional
natural frequency (Figure 4.4), showed an accurate match with the reported val-
==PAGE:99==
80
5 Conclusions, Recommendations & Future Work
ues of Euler-Bernoulli and Rayleigh rotors. For the case of shear effects values
and Timoshenko values, a decrease is observed with an increase of slenderness
ratio when compared to the reported results. However the shear effect com-
puted values show a perfect match with the results corresponding to the closed
form solutions, while the Timoshenko computed values fall between the reported
values and those of the closed form solution at higher slenderness ratios.
For the second case (simply supported rotating Timoshenko shaft), the com-
puted values of the first primary critical speeds (Figure 4.5) showed an exact
agreement with the reported values. This was also the case for the values of the
backward second primary critical speed (Figure 4.6). However, in the case for
the values of the forward second primary critical speed, the computed results
were smaller than the reported results when slenderness ratio was higher. The
above results indicate that the Timoshenko finite element model developed in
this thesis provides an accurate representation of rotating shaft dynamics. The
consideration of shear deformation made a more precise finite element.
• In the third step, the MATLAB script was modified to account for the effects
of the internal viscoelastic damping by using the formulation presented by Gan-
guly et al. (2018a). As it was done previously, the standard test case presented
in the latter publication was used for the theoretical validation of the script.
The standard test case studied the frequency response of a non-rotating simply
supported shaft for two different materials (ABS and PPC) and two different
disc configurations (Models I and II) when subjected to a periodic vertical force
of 1 N. It was only possible to make the validation partially, since the results
were provided in graphical form (Figures 3.11 and 3.12) only. From the analysis
it was found that the resonance peak for the four cases were within the range
of the resonance peaks from Figures 3.11 and 3.12. Further, the frequency re-
sponse curves in the four cases showed a consistent signature with those curves
presented in Figures 3.11 and 3.12, except in the range of ω < 500 rad/s where
the path of the computed frequency response curves did not match the path of
the curves from the standard test case.
• For the last stage of the finite element validation process, the script was set
to reproduce the dynamic response (theoretical and experimental) of the rotor-
bearing system presented by Molsen and Pedersen (2014) with the Euler-Bernoulli,
Timoshenko and Viscoelastic Finite Element Models. The match of the theoret-
ical results was straightforward: the critical speeds of the Euler-Bernoulli and
Timoshenko models were within a deviation of 2% from the reported values.
The critical speed values predicted with the Viscoelastic model had a deviation
of -6.72% and -3.92% for the backward and forward critical speeds, respectively.
The instability speed value was the same for the three models (3469 r.p.m),
==PAGE:100==
5.2 Recommendations & Future Work
81
with a deviation of 4.57% from the reported value. The best match with the
experimental results was achieved by setting the density of the measurement
disc material to ρalum = 2700
kg/m3, which reduced the load on the bearings,
and setting the clearance for both bearings to 55 µm, a value out of specification
and below the reported value. With these parameters the critical speed values
predicted with the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko Finite Element models had
a deviation in the range of 2.43% and -5.72% for the backward and forward crit-
ical speed values, respectively. The corresponding values for the Viscoelastic
Finite Element model were -7.68% and -12.02% for the backward and forward
critical speed, respectively. The experimental instability speed value was pre-
dicted exactly by the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko models, while the value
predicted by the Viscoelastic model was off by a margin of -9.67%.
• Finally, the adjusted rotor-bearing configuration from Molsen and Pedersen
(2014) and the bearing clearance were held to evaluate the Euler-Bernoulli,
Timoshenko and Viscoelastic Timoshenko dynamic response with a total of
23 different journal bearing configurations of L : D = 1.
In all cases, the
Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko rotor responses were identical due to the low
slenderness ratio (Nelson (1980)). The critical speed values for the Viscoelastic
model were smaller than those of the Euler-Bernoulli and Timoshenko models
for all bearing configurations. The unbalance response of the viscoelastic model
had an increasing behaviour for an increasing rotor rotation speed for all bearing
configurations, possibly due to overdamping.
To summarize, the previous observations indicate that the dynamic response of a
rotor can be approximated with acceptable accuracy with the Euler-Bernoulli or
Timoshenko Finite Element models. While the Timoshenko Finite Element model
constitutes a more complete approach, it is unnecessary when analyzing shafts with
a low slenderness ratio. In the case of the viscoelastic Finite Element Model, the val-
idation results were not conclusive since the available literature is limited. However,
it seemed to be suitable to model the rotor dynamic response of shafts built with
polymers or composite materials.
5.2
Recommendations & Future Work
To continue with the endeavours of this thesis, several actions may be developed for
future projects. These are
• Study the temperature effects in the viscosity of the bearing fluid introduced by
Molsen and Pedersen (2014) and their influence in the rotor dynamic response.
==PAGE:101==
82
5 Conclusions, Recommendations & Future Work
• Similarly, a study should be done to understand the contributions of the foun-
dations to the bearing stiffness and damping coefficients. And eventually, to
the overall rotor-bearing system dynamic response.
• Study the contribution of individual bearing characteristics and manufacturing
deviations to the rotor dynamic response.
• Determine the rotor-bearing system dynamic response experimentally for the
selected journal bearing configurations reported in section 4.3 of the present
thesis.
• Determine experimentally the properties of steel as a viscoelastic material (loss
modulus and storage modulus).

12
% DESCRIPTION: This
function computes the global matrices for a rotor of
13
% the type: 1) Euler-Bernoulli; 2) Timoshenko; 3) Viscoelastic based in the
14
% matrices presented in the papers listed below:
15
16
% 1) "The Dynamics of Rotor-Bearing Systems Using Finite Elements" by H.D.
Nelson,
17
% 1976.
18
%
19
% 2) "A Finite Shaft Element Using Timoshenko Beam Theory" by H.D.
20
% Nelson, 1980.
21
%
22
% 3) "A Viscoelastic Timoshenko Shaft Finite
23
% Element for Dynamic Analysis of Rotors" by S. Ganguly, 2016.
24
25
% INPUTS
26
% RotatorySwitch - Rotatory effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
27
% ShearSwitch
- Shear effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
28
% ViscoSwitch
- Viscoelastic effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
29
% NE
- Number of shaft elements
30
% GL
- Number of degrees of freedom
31
% E
- Modulus of elasticity
32
% SM
- Shear Modulus
33
% k
- Transverse shear factor
34
% E_branch
- Modulus of elasticity for parallel branches
35
% SM_branch
- Shear Modulus for parallel branches
36
% Eta_ep
- Strain damping parameter for parallel branches
37
% Eta_gam
- Shear damping parameter for parallel branches
38
% l
- Vector for length of the shaft elements
39
% rx
- Vector for external radius of shaft elements
40
% ri
- Vector for internal radius of shaft elements
41
% ro
- Vector for density of shaft elements
42
% St
- Vector for transversal area of the shaft elements
43
% II
- Vector for area moment of inertia of the shaft elements
44
% Omega
- Shaft spin speed
45
46
% OUTPUTS
47
% M=Mt+Mr
- Mass Matrix
48
% Mn=Mt-Mr;
49
% G
- Gyroscopic Matrix
50
% K
- Stiffness Matrix
51
% Ke
- Stiffness Matrix for parallel branches
52
% Kb
- Damping Matrix for parallel branches

- Identity Matrix size [GLxGL]

Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
153
Script used for the theoretical validation of the Timoshenko Finite
Element Model
1
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
2
% THESIS PROJECT - MSc IN ENGINEERING DESIGN AND APPLIED MECHANICS %
3
% MEK - DEPARTMENT OF MECHANICAL ENGINEERING
%
4
% DTU - TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
%
5
%

6

Lyngby - SPRING 2019

7

8

Salvador Gerardo Álvarez Villaseñor
%
9
%
%
10
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
11
12
% DESCRIPTION: This
function/script reproduces the results from
13
% the paper: "A Finite Shaft Element Using Timoshenko Beam Theory" by H.D.
14
% Nelson, 1980. Output options are vector of Non-dimensional frequency,
15
% Campbell's Diagram
16
17
clear all;
18
close all;
19
20
PartSwitch = 1; % Choose paper part: 1 - Part I; 2 - Part II
21
22
if PartSwitch == 1
23
24
CaseSwitch = [1 2 3 4]; % Load Configuration cases to compute
25
N_campbell = 1;
% Number of iterations
26
27
else
28
29
CaseSwitch = [4]; % Load Configuration cases to compute
30
N_campbell = 901; % Number of iterations
31
32
end
33
34
for mcase=1:length(CaseSwitch)
35
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
36
%
MODEL CONFIGURATION
%
37
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
38
39
ConfigSwitch = CaseSwitch(mcase);
% Set Model Configuration: 1 - Euler-
bernoulli; 2 - Rayleigh; 3 - Shear Effects Only; 4 - Timoshenko
40
ElementSwitch = 2;
% Set rotor discretization: 1 - 3
Elements; 2 - 5 Elements, 3 - 7 Elements
41
ViscoSwitch = 0;
% Viscoelastic effects On/Off: 0 - Off;
1 - On
42
43
switch ConfigSwitch
44
45
case 1 % Euler-Bernoulli
46
47
RotatorySwitch = 0; % Rotatory effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
48
ShearSwitch = 0;
% Shear effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
49
==PAGE:173==
154
Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
50
case 2 % Rayleigh - Rotatory effects Only
51
52
RotatorySwitch = 1; % Rotatory effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
53
ShearSwitch = 0;
% Shear effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
54
55
case 3 % Shear effects only
56
57
RotatorySwitch = 0; % Rotatory effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
58
ShearSwitch = 1;
% Shear effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
59
60
case 4 % Timoshenko
61
62
RotatorySwitch = 1; % Rotatory effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
63
ShearSwitch = 1;
% Shear effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On
64
65
end
66
67
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
68
% DEFINITION OF THE MODEL STRUCTURE
%
69
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
70
71
switch ElementSwitch
72
73
case 1 % 3 ELEMENTS DISCRETIZATION
74
75
NE=3;
% number of shaft elements
76
GL = (NE+1)*4; % number of degree of freedom
77
ND=0;
% number of discs
78
NM=2;
% number of bearings
79
CMM1=1;
% node - bearing 1
80
CMM2=4;
% node - bearing 2
81
82
ElementTag = '3E'; % Tag for output file name
83
84
case 2 % 5 ELEMENTS DISCRETIZATION
85
86
NE=5;
% number of shaft elements
87
GL = (NE+1)*4; % number of degree of freedom
88
ND=0;
% number of discs
89
NM=2;
% number of bearings
90
CMM1=1;
% node - bearing 1
91
CMM2=6;
% node - bearing 2
92
93
ElementTag = '5E'; % Tag for output file name
94
95
case 3 % 7 ELEMENTS DISCRETIZATION
96
97
NE=7;
% number of shaft elements
98
GL = (NE+1)*4; % number of degree of freedom
99
ND=1;
% number of discs
100
NM=2;
% number of bearings
101
CMM1=1;
% node - bearing 1
102
CMM2=8;
% node - bearing 2

ElementTag = '7E'; % Tag for output file name

Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
155
105
106
end
107
108
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
109
%
MATERIAL PROPERTIES
%
110
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
111
112
E = 2.078*10^11;
% Steel elasticity modulus [N/m^2]
113
RhoMat = 7806;
% Steel density [kg/m^3]
114
SM = E/3;
% Steel shear modulus [N/m^2]
115
k = 10/9;
% transverse shear factor} k=10/9 (circular cross-section)
according to Eshleman & Eubanks
116
117
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
118
% GEOMETRY OF THE ROTATING MACHINE
%
119
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
120
121
%(A) BEARINGS
122
123
Kty0=10^12;
% Bearing stiffness [N/m]
124
Ktz0=10^12;
% Bearing stiffness [N/m]
125
Kvw=0.0;
% Bearing
cross copupling stiffness
[N/m]
126
127
% Bearing 1 - Damping
128
Dty1 = 0.0 ;
129
Dtz1 = 0.0 ;
130
Dry1 = 0.0 ;
131
Drz1 = 0.0 ;
132
% Bearing 2 - Damping
133
Dty2 = 0.0 ;
134
Dtz2 = 0.0 ;
135
Dry2 = 0.0 ;
136
Drz2 = 0.0 ;
137
% Bearing 1 - Stiffness
138
Kty1 = Kty0 ;
139
Ktz1 = Ktz0 ;
140
Kry1 = Kvw ;
141
Krz1 = Kvw ;
142
% Bearing 2 - Stiffness
143
Kty2 = Kty0 ;
144
Ktz2 = Ktz0 ;
145
Kry2 = Kvw ;
146
Krz2 = Kvw ;
147
148
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
149
%
(B) SHAFT
%
150
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
151
152
ll = 0.5;
% Total length of shaft [m]
153
Rint = 0.0; % shaft internal radius [m]
154
SlRVec = 0.02*(1:1:5); % Load slenderness ratio cases to compute
(0.02/0.04/0.06/0.08/0.10)
155
156
for ncase=1:length(SlRVec)
==PAGE:175==
156
Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
157
158
SlR = SlRVec(ncase);
% Slenderness ratio
159
160
% length of the shaft elements [m]
161
162
for i=1:NE,
163
l(i) = ll/NE;
164
end
165
166
% external radius of shaft elements [m]
167
168
for i=1:NE,
169
rx(i) = 2*ll*SlR;
170
end
171
172
% internal radius of shaft elements [m]
173
174
for i=1:NE,
175
ri(i) = Rint;
176
end
177
178
% density of shaft elements [kg/m^3]
179
180
for i=1:NE,
181
ro(i) = RhoMat;
182
end
183
184
% transversal area of the shaft elements [m^2]
185
186
for i=1:NE,
187
St(i) = pi*(rx(i)^2-ri(i)^2);
188
end
189
190
% area moment of inertia of the shaft elements [m^4]
191
192
for i=1:NE,
193
II(i)=pi*(rx(i)^4-ri(i)^4)/4;
194
end
195
196
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
197
%
OPERATIONAL CONDITIONS
%
198
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
199
200
for iii=1:N_campbell,
201
Omega = 32*(iii-1)*2*pi; % angular velocity [rad/s]
202
Omegarad(iii) = Omega;
% angular velocity [rad/s]
203
Omegarpm(iii) = Omega*60/2/pi; % angular velocity [rpm]
204
205
[M,~,G,K,~,~,~,~]= GlobalMatrices(RotatorySwitch ,ShearSwitch ,ViscoSwitch ,NE,
GL,E,SM,k,1,1,1,1,l,rx,ri,ro,St,II,Omega);
206
207
% Adding the stiffness matrices of the bearing elements
208
209
K((CMM1-1)*4+1,(CMM1-1)*4+1)=K((CMM1-1)*4+1,(CMM1-1)*4+1)+Ktz1;
210

% DESCRIPTION: This
function/script reproduces the frequency response

% results (3.1) from the paper: "A Viscoelastic Timoshenko Shaft Finite

% Element for Dynamic Analysis of Rotors" by S. Ganguly, 2016.

for ncase=1:length(CaseSwitch)

Hydrodynamic bearings

% Shear effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On

% Viscoelastic effects On/Off: 0 - Off; 1 - On

% node - bearing 2

ND=1;

% Global Horizontal displacement
matrix

% Global Vertical displacement matrix
315
Xs(:,iii)=abs(xforw + conj(xback)); % Global displacement matrix

end

Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
Script of function used to calculate stiffness and damping coefficients for
Journal Bearings
1
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
2
% THESIS PROJECT - MSc IN ENGINEERING DESIGN AND APPLIED MECHANICS %
3
% MEK - DEPARTMENT OF MECHANICAL ENGINEERING
%
4
% DTU - TECHNICAL UNIVERSITY OF DENMARK
%
5
%
%
6
%
Lyngby - SPRING 2019
%
7
%
%
8
%
Salvador Gerardo Álvarez Villaseñor
%
9
%
%
10
%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%%
11
12
% DESCRIPTION: THIS FUNCTION READS THE SPECIFIED HYDRODYNAMIC BEARING
13
% PARAMETERS AND RETURNS THE STIFFNESS AND DAMPING COEFFICIENTS FOR THE
14
% SPECIFIED BEARING TABLE.
15
16
% INPUTS
17
% C
- Clearance
18
% r
- Journal/shaft radius
19
% W
- Bearing load
20
% eta
- Lubricant viscosity
21
% LB
- Bearing width
22
% Omega - Shaft spin speed
23
24
% OUTPUTS
25
% Stiffness coeff.: Kxx,Kxy,Kyx,Kyy [N/m]
26
% Damping coeff.: Dxx,Dxy,Dyx,Dyy [N*s/m]
27
% SoRef: Sommerfeld Number corresponding to the given angular velocity [-]
28
% OmegaMin: Minimum spin speed [rad/s]
29
30
function [Kxx,Kxy,Kyx,Kyy,Dxx,Dxy,Dyx,Dyy,SoRef,OmegaMin]= hydrobearing(C,r,
W,eta,LB,Omega)
31
32
JournalBearingTables % Load Bearing Tables (Someya)
33
34
BearingTable = Calc_02; % Points to desired Hydrodynamic Bearing Table
35
36
% Bearing parameters
37
38
R = r+C;
% Bearing radius [m]
39
N = Omega/2/pi; % Spin speed [Hz]
40
41
% Sommerfeld Number corresponding to Omega
42
SoRef = ((2*eta*N*LB*R)/(W))*((R/C)^2);
43
44
% Minimum spin speed [rad/s]
45
OmegaMin = 2*pi*((BearingTable(size(BearingTable ,1),1)*W)/(eta*LB*2*R))
*((C/R)^2);
46
47
% Loop sets operation limits according to bearing table
48
49
% Sets values of stiffness and
damping coeff. to table minimums when
spin speed is zero
==PAGE:210==
Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
191
50
if SoRef < BearingTable(size(BearingTable ,1),1)
51
52
SoRef = BearingTable(size(BearingTable ,1),1);
53
Omega = OmegaMin;
54
55
% Stops model when spin speed exceeds bearing table limit
56
elseif SoRef > BearingTable(1,1)
57
58
error('Shaft spin speed exceeds bearing table values.')
59
60
return
61
62
% Mantains computed Sommerfeld number
63
else
64
65
SoRef = SoRef;
66
67
end
68
69
%Interpolating Stiffness and Damping Coefficients from bearing tables
70
71
Kxx_So = (W/C)*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,7),SoRef);
72
Kxy_So = (W/C)*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,8),SoRef);
73
Kyx_So = (W/C)*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,9),SoRef);
74
Kyy_So = (W/C)*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,10),SoRef);
75
76
Dxx_So = (W/(C*Omega))*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,11),
SoRef);
77
Dxy_So = (W/(C*Omega))*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,12),
SoRef);
78
Dyx_So = (W/(C*Omega))*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,13),
SoRef);
79
Dyy_So = (W/(C*Omega))*interp1(BearingTable(:,1),BearingTable(:,14),
SoRef);
80
81
% Switch Stiffness and Damping coefficients reference frames
82
83
% Stifness coefficient matrix in Someya Ref. Frame
84
K_So = [Kxx_So Kxy_So 0
85
Kyx_So Kyy_So 0
86
0
0
0];
87
88
% Damping coefficient matrix in Someya Ref. Frame
89
D_So = [Dxx_So Dxy_So 0
90
Dyx_So Dyy_So 0
91
0
0
0];
92
93
% Transformation Matrix between Someya and Shaft Reference Frame
94
Angle = -90; % Rotating angle [º]
95
Ry = [cosd(Angle) 0 sind(Angle); 0 1 0; -sind(Angle) 0 cosd(Angle)];
96
97
% Stifness coefficients in shaft reference frame
98
99
K_Shaft = (Ry')*K_So*(Ry); % Sets stiffness coefficients from bearing
ref. frame to shaft ref. frame
==PAGE:211==
192
Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
100
101
Kxx = K_Shaft(3,3);
102
Kxy = K_Shaft(3,2);
103
Kyx = K_Shaft(2,3);
104
Kyy = K_Shaft(2,2);
105
106
% Damping coefficients in shaft reference frame
107
108
D_Shaft = Ry*D_So*Ry'; % Sets damping coefficients from bearing ref.
frame to shaft ref. frame
109
110
Dxx = D_Shaft(3,3);
111
Dxy = D_Shaft(3,2);
112
Dyx = D_Shaft(2,3);
113
Dyy = D_Shaft(2,2);
114
115
end

function [NER,GLR,KR,MR,GR,T,Kss]=GuyanReduction(K,M,G,SlaveDofs)
13
14
%
Guyan reduction or Static reduction
15
%
with this method, the reduced model tries to reproduce
16
%
the natural freq.s of full model.
17
%
18
% K , M , D: Full order stiffness & Mass Matrices
19
% SlaveDofs : Dofs to be deattached from system
20
% KR, MR,Dr : Reduced stiffness & Mass Matrices
21
% T : Transformation Matrix
22
% Kss : Slave Stiffness Matrix
23
%
24
% Example:
25
% k = [2 3 5;6 7 8;3 2 1];
26
% [kr,~,~,~,Tr,~] = GuyanReduction(k,k,k,1)
27
%
28
% Inspired by http://www.mathworks.com/matlabcentral/fileexchange/61877-
guyan-reduction
29
% And by http://www.mathworks.com/matlabcentral/fileexchange/12504-guyan-
reduction
30
31
Dof = length(K(:,1));
32
SlaveDofs = sort(SlaveDofs);
33
index = 1:Dof;
34
35
index(SlaveDofs) = [];
36
for ii = 1:length(SlaveDofs)
37
for ij = 1:length(SlaveDofs)
38
Kss(ii,ij) = K(SlaveDofs(ii),SlaveDofs(ij));
39
end
40
end
41
42
for ii = 1 : length(SlaveDofs)
43
for ij = 1 : length(index)
44
Ksm(ii,ij) =
K(SlaveDofs(ii),index(ij));
45
end
46
end
47
48
P = - inv(Kss) * Ksm;
49
T = zeros(length(M),length(index));
50
T(SlaveDofs ,:) = P;
==PAGE:233==
214
Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
51
T(index ,:) = eye(length(index));
52
GLR=size(T,2); % Number of DoFs for reduced matrices
53
NER= (GLR/4)-1;% Number of Elements for reduced matrices
54
MR = T'*M*T;
55
KR = T'*K*T;
56
GR = T'*G*T;
57
58
end

Third party script of function used to find the intersection of two lines -
By Douglas M. Schwarz
1
function [x0,y0,iout,jout] = intersections(x1,y1,x2,y2,robust)
2
%INTERSECTIONS Intersections of curves.
3
%
Computes the (x,y) locations where two curves intersect.
The curves
4
%
can be broken with NaNs or have vertical segments.
5
%
6
% Example:
7
%
[X0,Y0] = intersections(X1,Y1,X2,Y2,ROBUST);
8
%
9
% where X1 and Y1 are equal-length vectors of at least two points and
10
% represent curve 1.
Similarly, X2 and Y2 represent curve 2.
11
% X0 and Y0 are column vectors containing the points at which the two
12
% curves intersect.
13
%
14
% ROBUST (optional) set to 1 or true means to use a slight variation of the
15
% algorithm that might return duplicates of some intersection points, and
16
% then remove those duplicates.
The default is true, but since the
17
% algorithm is slightly slower you can set it to false if you know that
18
% your curves don't intersect at any segment boundaries.
Also, the robust
19
% version properly handles parallel and overlapping segments.
20
%
21
% The algorithm can return two additional vectors that indicate which
22
% segment pairs contain intersections and where they are:
23
%
24
%
[X0,Y0,I,J] = intersections(X1,Y1,X2,Y2,ROBUST);
25
%
26
% For each element of the vector I, I(k) = (segment number of (X1,Y1)) +
27
% (how far along this segment the intersection is).
For example, if I(k) =
28
% 45.25 then the intersection lies a quarter of the way between the line
29
% segment connecting (X1(45),Y1(45)) and (X1(46),Y1(46)).
Similarly for
30
% the vector J and the segments in (X2,Y2).
31
%
32
% You can also get intersections of a curve with itself.
Simply pass in
33
% only one curve, i.e.,
34
%
35
%
[X0,Y0] = intersections(X1,Y1,ROBUST);
36
%
37
% where, as before, ROBUST is optional.
38
39
% Version: 2.0, 25 May 2017
40
% Author:
Douglas M. Schwarz
41
% Email:
dmschwarz=ieee*org, dmschwarz=urgrad*rochester*edu
42
% Real_email = regexprep(Email,{'=','*'},{'@','.'})
43
44
45
% Theory of operation:
46
%
47
% Given two line segments, L1 and L2,
48
%
49
%
L1 endpoints:
(x1(1),y1(1)) and (x1(2),y1(2))
50
%
L2 endpoints:
(x2(1),y2(1)) and (x2(2),y2(2))
51
%
52
% we can write four equations with four unknowns and then solve them.
The
==PAGE:235==
216
Appendix D: Matlab Scripts
53
% four unknowns are t1, t2, x0 and y0, where (x0,y0) is the intersection of
54
% L1 and L2, t1 is the distance from the starting point of L1 to the
55
% intersection relative to the length of L1 and t2 is the distance from the
56
% starting point of L2 to the intersection relative to the length of L2.
57
%
58
% So, the four equations are
59
%
60
%
(x1(2) - x1(1))*t1 = x0 - x1(1)
61
%
(x2(2) - x2(1))*t2 = x0 - x2(1)
62
%
(y1(2) - y1(1))*t1 = y0 - y1(1)
63
%
(y2(2) - y2(1))*t2 = y0 - y2(1)
64
%
65
% Rearranging and writing in matrix form,
66
%
67
%
[x1(2)-x1(1)
0
-1
0;
[t1;
[-x1(1);
68
%
0
x2(2)-x2(1)
-1

t2;
=

y1(2)-y1(1)

0
-1;
x0;
-y1(1);
70
%
0
y2(2)-y2(1)
0
-1]
y0]
-y2(1)]
71
%
72
% Let's call that A*T = B.
We can solve for T with T = A\B.
73
%
74
% Once we have our solution we just have to look at t1 and t2 to determine
75
% whether L1 and L2 intersect.
If 0 <= t1 < 1 and 0 <= t2 < 1 then the two
76
% line segments cross and we can include (x0,y0) in the output.
77
%
78
% In principle, we have to perform this computation on every pair of line
79
% segments in the input data.
This can be quite a large number of pairs so
80
% we will reduce it by doing a simple preliminary check to eliminate line
81
% segment pairs that could not possibly cross.
The check is to look at the
82
% smallest enclosing rectangles (with sides parallel to the axes) for each
83
% line segment pair and see if they overlap.
If they do then we have to
84
% compute t1 and t2 (via the A\B computation) to see if the line segments
85
% cross, but if they don't then the line segments cannot cross.
In a
86
% typical application , this technique will eliminate most of the potential
87
% line segment pairs.
88

154

repmat(mvmax(y1),1,n2) >= repmat(mvmin(y2).',n1,1));
155
156

157
% segments in each curve.
We want to avoid forming large matrices for
158

If the matrices are not too large,
